                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequences of two diploid wild relatives of cultivated sweetpotato reveal targets for genetic improvement"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-11-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.] is a globally important staple food crop, especially for sub-Saharan Africa. Agronomic improvement of sweetpotato has lagged behind other major food crops due to a lack of genomic and genetic resources and inherent challenges in breeding a heterozygous, clonally propagated polyploid. Here, we report the genome sequences of its two diploid relatives, I. trifida and I. triloba, and show that these high-quality genome assemblies are robust references for hexaploid sweetpotato. Comparative and phylogenetic analyses reveal insights into the ancient whole-genome triplication history of Ipomoea and evolutionary relationships within the Batatas complex. Using resequencing data from 16 genotypes widely used in African breeding programs, genes and alleles associated with carotenoid biosynthesis in storage roots are identified, which may enable efficient breeding of varieties with high provitamin A content. These resources will facilitate genome-enabled breeding in this important food security crop. Sweetpotato is an important food security crop providing rich source of macro- and micronutrients including carbohydrates and vitamins. Here, the authors assemble of the two diploid relatives of cultivated sweetpotato and identify genes and alleles associated with carotenoid biosynthesis from breeding lines."/>

Robin Buell<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a>,<a href="#Aff15">15</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Michigan State University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2150 1785, grid.17088.36, Department of Plant Biology, Michigan State University, East Lansing, MI, 48824, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Michigan State University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2150 1785, grid.17088.36, Plant Resilience Institute, Michigan State University, East Lansing, MI, 48824, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Zhangjun-Fei" data-author-popup="auth-Zhangjun-Fei" data-corresp-id="c2">Zhangjun Fei<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-9684-1450"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-9684-1450</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff16">16</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Cornell University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="000000041936877X, grid.5386.8, Boyce Thompson Institute, Cornell University, Ithaca, NY, 14853, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="USDA-ARS Robert W. Holley Center for Agriculture and Health" /><meta itemprop="address" content="USDA-ARS Robert W. Holley Center for Agriculture and Health, Ithaca, NY, 14853, USA" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sweetpotato [<i>Ipomoea batatas</i> (L.) Lam.] is a globally important staple food crop, especially for sub-Saharan Africa. Agronomic improvement of sweetpotato has lagged behind other major food crops due to a lack of genomic and genetic resources and inherent challenges in breeding a heterozygous, clonally propagated polyploid. Here, we report the genome sequences of its two diploid relatives, <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, and show that these high-quality genome assemblies are robust references for hexaploid sweetpotato. Comparative and phylogenetic analyses reveal insights into the ancient whole-genome triplication history of <i>Ipomoea</i> and evolutionary relationships within the Batatas complex. Using resequencing data from 16 genotypes widely used in African breeding programs, genes and alleles associated with carotenoid biosynthesis in storage roots are identified, which may enable efficient breeding of varieties with high provitamin A content. These resources will facilitate genome-enabled breeding in this important food security crop.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sweetpotato, <i>Ipomoea batatas</i> (L.) Lam. (2<i>n</i> = 6<i>x</i> = 90), is an important food crop ranking seventh globally with 106.6 million tons produced in 2014 (FAOSTAT 2014). Sweetpotato provides a rich source of carbohydrates, dietary fiber, vitamins, and micronutrients, is low in fat and cholesterol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Loebenstein, G. in The Sweetpotato (eds Loebenstein, G. &amp; Thottappilly, G. eds.) 9–12 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1079">1</a></sup>, and due to its resilience and adaptability, it serves an important role in food security for subsistence farmers in sub-Saharan Africa (SSA). Indeed, efforts by the International Potato Center (CIP) to replace the dominant β-carotene lacking white-fleshed varieties in SSA with provitamin A-rich orange-fleshed sweetpotato (OFSP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Labarta, R. in Crop Improvement, Adoption, and Impact of Improved Varieties in Food Crops in Sub-Saharan Africa (eds. Walker, T. S. &amp; Alwang, J.) 164–182 (CABI, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1083">2</a></sup> were considered a breakthrough achievement as vitamin A deficiency affects more than 40% of children under 5 years old in SSA and is a leading cause of blindness and premature death<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Low, J. W., Mwanga, R. O. M., Andrade, M., Carey, E. &amp; Ball, A.-M. Tackling vitamin A deficiency with biofortified sweetpotato in sub-Saharan Africa. Glob. Food Sec. 14, 23–30 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1088">3</a></sup>. Awarding of the World Food Prize in 2016 to four scientists who pioneered biofortification with OFSP highlights the significance of these efforts and the importance of OFSP in shifting human health outcomes.</p><p>Sweetpotato was domesticated in tropical America at least 5000 years ago in a region between the Yucatan Peninsula of Mexico and the Orinoco River in Venezuela<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Austin, D. F. in Exploration, Maintenance and Utilization of Sweet Potato Genetic Resources. Report of the First Sweet Potato Planning Conference (International Potato Centre (CIP), Lima, Peru, 1987) (ed. P., G.) 27–59 (CIP, 1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1095">4</a></sup>, and introduced to Europe and Africa from South America by Europeans in the early 16th century and later to the rest of the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Roullier, C., Benoit, L., McKey, D. B. &amp; Lebot, V. Historical collections reveal patterns of diffusion of sweet potato in Oceania obscured by modern plant movements and recombination. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 2205–2210 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1099">5</a></sup>. Whereas sweetpotato has long been thought to have been introduced to Oceania by Polynesians in pre-Columbian times<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Roullier, C., Benoit, L., McKey, D. B. &amp; Lebot, V. Historical collections reveal patterns of diffusion of sweet potato in Oceania obscured by modern plant movements and recombination. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 2205–2210 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1103">5</a></sup>, this hypothesis has recently been challenged<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1107">6</a></sup>. Sweetpotato is one of only two food crops (the other being water spinach, <i>I. aquatic</i> Forssk.) among ~1600 species in the morning glory family (Convolvulaceae). The wild diploid species <i>I. trifida</i> (Kunth.) G. Don (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 30) has been widely reported as the closest relative of cultivated sweetpotato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1124">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Roullier, C. et al. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam. PLoS ONE 8, e62707 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1127">7</a></sup>, but there are two hypotheses regarding the origin of the cultivated hexaploid sweetpotato: an autopolyploidization within an <i>I. trifida</i> progenitor population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1134">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Roullier, C. et al. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam. PLoS ONE 8, e62707 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1137">7</a></sup> or an allo-autopolyploidization involving both <i>I. trifida</i> and another diploid wild relative, possibly <i>Ipomoea triloba</i> L. (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 30)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Austin, D. F. in Exploration, Maintenance and Utilization of Sweet Potato Genetic Resources. Report of the First Sweet Potato Planning Conference (International Potato Centre (CIP), Lima, Peru, 1987) (ed. P., G.) 27–59 (CIP, 1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1154">4</a></sup>. Some accessions of <i>I. trifida</i> are sexually compatible with <i>I. batatas</i>, though this is rare and, unlike <i>I. triloba</i>, commonly produces unreduced pollen associated with polyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Orjeda, G., Freyre, R. &amp; Iwanaga, M. Production of 2n pollen in diploid Ipomoea trifida, a putative wild ancestor of sweet potato. J. Hered. 81, 462–467 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1168">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Becerra Lopez-Lavalle, L. A. &amp; Orjeda, G. Occurrence and cytological mechanism of 2n pollen formation in a tetraploid accession of Ipomoea batatas (sweet potato). J. Hered. 93, 185–192 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1171">9</a></sup>. Observations of a predominance of polysomic inheritance have been interpreted as support for the autopolyploidization hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kriegner, A., Cervantes, J. C., Burg, K., Mwanga, R. O. M. &amp; Zhang, D. A genetic linkage map of sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.] based on AFLP markers. Mol. Breed. 11, 169–185 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1175">10</a></sup>. However, frequent tetrasomic recombination has been observed in allopolyploids, such as cultivated peanut (<i>Arachis hypogaea</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Leal-Bertioli, S. et al. Tetrasomic recombination is surprisingly frequent in allotetraploid Arachis. Genetics 199, 1093–1105 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1182">11</a></sup>. Some molecular data implicate <i>I. trifida</i> as a close, but not direct ancestor, and provide no evidence for <i>I. triloba</i> as contributing to the hexaploid genome of sweetpotato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Roullier, C. et al. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam. PLoS ONE 8, e62707 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1193">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Srisuwan, S., Sihachakr, D. &amp; Siljak-Yakovlev, S. The origin and evolution of sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas Lam.) and its wild relatives through the cytogenetic approaches. Plant Sci. 171, 424–433 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1196">12</a></sup>. Roullier et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Roullier, C. et al. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam. PLoS ONE 8, e62707 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1200">7</a></sup> speculate that sweetpotato could share a common ancestor with <i>I. trifida</i> and/or tetraploid <i>I. batatas</i> yet have multiple origins with at least two autopolyploidization events from a single progenitor species. Munoz-Rodrıguez et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1211">6</a></sup> recently used targeted gene capture to analyze hexaploid <i>I. batatas</i> and wild relatives in an attempt to resolve the origin of the hexaploid sweetpotato, but their findings have yet to be tested using whole-genome sequence data.</p><p>The improvement of sweetpotato varieties across the world faces major constraints due to the lack of knowledge of the genetic, molecular, and physiological basis of key agronomic traits of this critical food crop. The sweetpotato genome is large (1.6 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species nuclear DNA content material and methods. Plant Mol. Biol. Report. 9, 208–218 (1991)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1221">13</a></sup> and complex due to polyploidy and a high degree of heterozygosity, challenging efforts to generate a high-quality de novo genome assembly. While an assembly of <i>I. trifida</i> was released in 2015, it was highly fragmented<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 22, 171–179 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1228">14</a></sup>, and thus its use as a reference for genome-enabled breeding efforts in sweetpotato is limited. Likewise, a recently reported haplotype-resolved genome assembly of <i>I. batatas</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, J. et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat. Plants 3, 696–703 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1234">15</a></sup> is incomplete and contains a considerable amount of redundancy and misassemblies, including erroneous assembly of haplotypes. To provide high-quality reference genome resources and further investigate the relationship between hexaploid sweetpotato and its diploid relatives, we generated high quality, chromosome-scale genome assemblies of <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, resequenced 16 cultivars and landraces widely used in African breeding programs, and performed a suite of comparative genomic analyses. Our findings illustrate the utility of high-quality diploid <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genomes for genome-enabled breeding and research in this important food security crop.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assemblies of <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>
                        </h3><p>We sequenced the genomes of <i>I. trifida</i> NCNSP0306 and <i>I. triloba</i> NCNSP0323, two diploid species closely related to the hexaploid sweetpotato (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>). A total of 104.8 and 144.1 Gb high-quality cleaned Illumina paired-end and mate-pair reads were generated for <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, respectively, representing 199× and 291× coverage of their genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>). Based on the 17-mer depth distribution of the Illumina reads (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>), the <i>I. trifida</i> genome is 526.4 Mb with a heterozygosity level of 0.24% calculated from single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) analyses. In contrast, <i>I. triloba</i> NCNSP0323 is highly homozygous (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>) with an estimated genome size of 495.9 Mb. Long-read PacBio sequence data (11× and 5× for <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, respectively; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>) was used for gap-filling and used in combination with de novo-assembled BioNano genome maps to improve the assembly (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">2</a>). The resulting high-quality assemblies for <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> were 462.0 and 457.8 Mb with scaffold N50 lengths of 1.2 and 6.9 Mb, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="461" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Morphology of <i>Ipomoea batatas</i>, <i>I. trifida</i>, and <i>I. triloba</i>. <b>a</b>, <b>b</b> Flowers (<b>a</b>) and roots (<b>b</b>) of <i>I. batatas</i> “Taizhong6”, <i>I. trifida</i> NCNSP0306, and <i>I. triloba</i> NCNSP0323</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To construct pseudomolecules, a high-density genetic map for <i>I. trifida</i> was generated using an F<sub>1</sub> population derived from a cross between two heterozygous <i>I. trifida</i> lines, M9 and M19 (Supplementary Method 3 and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">2</a>). In all, 461 <i>I. trifida</i> scaffolds with a cumulative size of 373.4 Mb (80.8% of the assembly) were anchored to 15 linkage groups (LGs), of which 196 (66.3%) were oriented (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">3</a>). The <i>I. trifida</i> genetic map was also used to anchor 216 <i>I. triloba</i> scaffolds (443.3 Mb; 96.8% of the assembly) to the 15 LGs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>To evaluate the quality of the assemblies, RNA-Seq reads from a set of developmental tissues were aligned to their cognate assemblies; 85.3% and 91.4% of the reads aligned to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genomes, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM4">1</a>). The completeness of the assemblies in terms of gene content was assessed with BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1418">16</a></sup>. Of the core conserved plant genes, 93.9% and 94.6% were complete in the <i>I. trifida</i> and the <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies, respectively, while another 1.9% and 1.6%, respectively, were fragmented. Collectively, these results demonstrate that our genome assemblies are of high quality.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat sequences characterization and genome annotation</h3><p>Repeat masking with species-specific custom repeat libraries revealed 217.4 Mb (50.2%) of the nongap sequence of the <i>I. trifida</i> assembly and 231.1 Mb (52.8%) of the <i>I. triloba</i> assembly was repetitive (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM5">2</a>). For <i>I. trifida</i>, the high-confidence gene set consists of 32,301 protein-coding genes represented by 44,158 gene models with an average transcript length of 1662.9 bp and average coding sequence (CDS) length of 1292.9 bp, of which 39,127 (88.6%) were assigned putative functions. For <i>I. triloba</i>, the high-confidence gene set consists of 31,423 protein-coding genes represented by 47,088 gene models with an average transcript length of 1784.5 bp and an average CDS length of 1322.7 bp, of which 41,639 (88.4%) were assigned putative functions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative genomics and lineage-specific family expansion</h3><p>We performed comparative analyses using 43,296 protein sequences derived from a transcriptome assembly of <i>I. batatas</i> “Beauregard” (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">4</a>) and the predicted proteomes from <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba</i>, and seven other representative plant species, including <i>I. nil</i> (Japanese morning glory), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (tomato), <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> (potato), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (grapevine), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (model eudicot), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (rice), and <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> (basal angiosperm). A total of 274,278 proteins from the 10 species (79.3% of the input sequences) clustered into 19,901 orthologous groups, of which, 3269 were present only in the four <i>Ipomoea</i> species, and 1680 were specific to the Batatas complex (<i>I. batatas</i>, <i>I. trifida</i>, and <i>I. triloba</i>) and absent in <i>I. nil</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig2">2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="668" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene family clustering and <i>Ipomoea</i> whole-genome triplication. <b>a</b> Venn diagram of orthologous gene families. OGs, orthologous groups. ‘Others’ include tomato, potato, grapevine, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, rice and <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>. <b>b</b> Distribution of <i>K</i>s of orthologous or paralogous genes within and between genomes of <i>I. trifida</i> (Itf), <i>I. triloba</i> (Itb), <i>I. nil</i> (Inl), tomato (Sly) and potato (Stu). Estimated times of speciation and WGT events were calculated using a mutation rate of 7 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per site per year. Mya, million years ago. <b>c</b> Multiplicated genomic regions in <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba</i>, <i>I. nil</i>, and tomato</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>From orthologous gene clustering with other angiosperm proteomes, 73 gene families (orthologous groups) were significantly expanded in <i>I. trifida</i> and/or <i>I. triloba</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM6">3</a>). This lineage-specific expansion gene set was enriched with biological processes including “response to stress”, “cell death”, “response to external stimulus”, and “response to biotic stimulus” (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001, Fisher’s exact test; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM7">4</a>). It is worth noting that two orthologous groups containing genes encoding sporamin proteins were expanded in both <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>. Sporamin, a Kunitz-type trypsin inhibitor (KTI), is a major storage protein in sweetpotato storage roots, and is analogous to patatin in potato which plays an important role in storage, defense, and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Senthilkumar, R. &amp; Yeh, K.-W. Multiple biological functions of sporamin related to stress tolerance in sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas Lam). Biotechnol. Adv. 30, 1309–1317 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1600">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1603">18</a></sup>. Based on the presence of the Kunitz soybean trypsin inhibitor domain (PF00197), we identified 94 and 83 putative KTIs in <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, respectively, whereas 41, 36, and 16 were present in <i>I. nil</i>, potato, and tomato, respectively, and only seven, five, one, and two were found in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape, rice, and <i>Amborella</i>, respectively. Phylogenetic relationships of KTIs in these nine species revealed a subgroup of KTIs specific to <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, as well as groups of expanded KTIs in potato (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">3</a>); suggestive of lineage-specific adaptation events in these root and tuber crops. In particular, two <i>I. batatas</i> sporamin genes, orthologous to the <i>I. trifida</i> genes <i>itf10g10920</i> and <i>itf01g01870</i> that are within the <i>Ipomoea-</i>lineage-specific expansion, were upregulated during storage root development in “Beauregard”, with expression levels 13–74-fold higher in storage roots compared to fibrous roots (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM8">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">
                           <i>Ipomoea</i> whole-genome triplication</h3><p>We identified syntenic blocks within the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genomes and inferred whole-genome multiplication events based on the distributions of pairwise synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i>s) of paralogous genes. The <i>K</i>s distributions in both <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> showed a peak at 0.65 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig2">2b</a>). A substantial proportion of the extant <i>Ipomoea</i> genomes occurred in triplicated blocks (23.5%, 34.8%, and 28.0% in <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba</i>, and <i>I. nil</i>, respectively), and these proportions are higher than that observed in the tomato genome (6.0%) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig2">2c</a>), which bears a signature of whole-genome triplication (WGT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1701">19</a></sup>. We further compared the genomes of <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba,</i> and <i>I. nil</i>, respectively, with the grape genome. A total of 72.9% of the <i>I. trifida</i> gene models are in syntenic blocks corresponding to one grape region. These grape regions collectively cover 79.9% of the grape gene space, among which 36.1% have three orthologous regions in <i>I. trifida</i>, 27.1% have two, and 16.8% have one. Similar results were obtained for <i>I. triloba</i> and <i>I. nil</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">4</a>). Based on the syntenic blocks involving single grape genome segments, each of the <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba</i>, and <i>I. nil</i> genomes can be partitioned into three nonoverlapping “subgenomes” (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">4</a>). Our data indicate that WGT occurred in an ancient ancestor of the <i>Ipomoea</i> lineage. While both the tomato and <i>Ipomoea</i> WGT events were indicated by peaks of <i>K</i>s near 0.65, the <i>K</i>s distribution for tomato, and <i>I. trifida</i> orthologous gene pairs is shifted to the right of the paralog-pair distributions for either species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig2">2b</a>), indicating that independent WGTs occurred in the ancestry of <i>Ipomoea</i> and Solanaceae after their divergence. Based on the evolutionary rate of 7.0 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions/site/year reported in both Solanaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Moniz de Sa, M. &amp; Drouin, G. Phylogeny and substitution rates of angiosperm actin genes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 13, 1198–1212 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1767">20</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1774">21</a></sup>, the <i>Ipomoea</i> WGT event was estimated to occur around 46.1 million years ago (Mya), much earlier than divergence of <i>I. nil</i> from the lineage containing <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> (~3.6 Mya), and the <i>I. trifida</i>-<i>I. triloba</i> divergence (~2.2 Mya). Comparison between the genomes of <i>I. trifida</i> (or <i>I. triloba</i>) and <i>I. nil</i> revealed one-to-one syntenic relationships for all 15 chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">5</a>), suggesting limited large-scale interchromosomal rearrangements over the last 3.6 million years.</p><p>A robust gene expression atlas of developmental and stress/hormone treatments (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">4</a>) was used to investigate the fate of gene copies following the ancient WGT event. Genes retained in triplicate or duplicate in the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genomes were significantly enriched (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001, Fisher’s exact test) for transcripts that exhibited up-regulation in response to abiotic and biotic stressors, suggesting WGT may play a role in adaptive stress responses. WGT genes were also enriched with gene ontologies suggestive of regulatory functions including “DNA binding”, “transcription factor activity”, “protein binding”, “kinase activity”, and “translation regulator activity” (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001, Fisher’s exact test; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM7">4</a>). For example, two sweetpotato knotted-like homeobox genes, <i>IBKN2</i> (orthologous to <i>itf01g32840</i>) and <i>IBKN3</i> (<i>itf01g32840</i>) known to be associated with storage root development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tanaka, M., Kato, N., Nakayama, H., Nakatani, M. &amp; Takahata, Y. Expression of class I knotted1-like homeobox genes in the storage roots of sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). J. Plant Physiol. 165, 1726–1735 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1843">22</a></sup>, were duplicated in the ancestral <i>Ipomoea</i> WGT event. In ‘Beauregard’, these two genes were expressed at higher levels in storage roots as compared with fibrous roots and were upregulated during the development of storage roots (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">6</a>), suggesting that the WGT contributed to additional gene copies that function in storage root development.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Utility of diploid genomes as references for sweetpotato</h3><p>Yang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, J. et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat. Plants 3, 696–703 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6145e1863">15</a></sup> recently presented an 836-Mb haplotype-resolved genome assembly of <i>I. batatas</i> “Taizhong6”. We assessed the quality of the “Taizhong6” assembly using multiple approaches and found the assembly was incomplete and contained numerous misassemblies, limiting its use as a reference for hexaploid sweetpotato (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">12</a>). To evaluate the utility of our two <i>Ipomoea</i> diploid genomes as references for hexaploid sweetpotato, we generated ~60× whole-genome sequence data for the hexaploid African landrace “Tanzania” using the 10× Genomics Chromium system (Supplementary Method 6) and aligned the linked reads to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies separately; ~83.5% of the reads could be aligned to both assemblies while ~7.7% of the reads did not align (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig3">3a</a>). Approximately, 5.4% and 3.4% of the reads aligned solely to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig3">3a</a>). Based on the alignment scores, ~57.7% of the reads aligned better to the <i>I. trifida</i> genome than the <i>I. triloba</i> genome, while ~31.9% of the reads aligned better in the reciprocal direction (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="925" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Comparative genomic analysis of hexaploid sweetpotato and two wild relatives. <b>a</b> Percentages of the mapped and unmapped 10× Genomics linked reads of hexaploid sweetpotato cultivar “Tanzania” to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies. <b>b</b> Percentages of 10× Genomics reads with better alignments when mapped to one genome assembly compared to the other. <b>c</b> Comparison of the hexaploid sweetpotato molecules with the two diploid assemblies. The outermost circle displays ideograms of the pseudochromosomes of the genome assemblies (in Mb scales). The <i>I. trifida</i> genome is on the left and the <i>I. triloba</i> genome is on the right. The second circle displays the normalized depth of coverage by 10× Genomics reads (1 Mb window). The third circle displays the average read depth of coverage of regions specifically homologous to <i>I. trifida</i> or <i>I. triloba</i> genomes (1 Mb window). The fourth circle displays the total length of specific regions (1 Mb window size). The fifth circle displays the percentage of the homologous sequences shared among the hexaploid genome and the two diploid genomes (1 Mb window size). The innermost circle displays homologies among the hexaploid and two diploid genomes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Molecules were reconstructed from the linked reads of “Tanzania” using their alignments to the two diploid genome assemblies. Approximately, 4.3 and 4.8 million “Tanzania” molecules of at least 10 kb were identified from alignments to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies, respectively, covering ~409 Mb (83%) and ~396 Mb (86%) of the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I.</i> <i>triloba</i> genomes. We identified 390,303 regions in “Tanzania” that were homologous to both diploid genomes, covering ~215 Mb (44%) and ~231 Mb (50%) of the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig3">3c</a>). We further identified regions that were specific to <i>I. trifida</i> (~7.0 Mb) or <i>I. triloba</i> (~9.9 Mb) genomes, suggesting that the hexaploid sweetpotato genome contains sequences that are uniquely shared with either of these two diploid species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig3">3c</a>). Collectively, our results suggest that the two diploid genome assemblies can serve as robust reference sequences for hexaploid sweetpotato as suggested by the high percentages (&gt;90%) of hexaploid reads aligned to either <i>I. trifida</i> or <i>I. triloba</i>. The <i>I. trifida</i> genome is a slightly better reference, while the <i>I. triloba</i> genome shares unique features with the hexaploid genome and can serve as a complement to the <i>I. trifida</i> genome.</p><p>We further analyzed the frequency of the <i>I. trifida</i>-type and <i>I. triloba</i>-type alleles in “Tanzania”. A total of 7.6 million SNP and indel variants were identified between the <i>I. trifida</i> and the <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies, of which, approximately 5.6 million variant sites had at least 10× depth of coverage from the 10× Genomics data of “Tanzania”. The majority of these sites (~73.9%) were nonpolymorphic in the “Tanzania” genome comprising both <i>I. trifida</i>-specific (~46.2%) and <i>I. triloba-</i>specific (~27.7%) homozygous sites (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">13a</a>). This observation indicated that both <i>I. trifida</i>-like and <i>I. triloba</i>-like progenitors had contributed to the “Tanzania” genome, and the <i>I. trifida</i>-like progenitor had contributed approximately twice as much as the <i>I. triloba</i>-like progenitor. The similar allele depth distribution for the <i>I. trifida</i>-specific and <i>I. triloba</i>-specific homozygous sites (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">13b</a>) in the hexaploid “Tanzania” genome suggested that it was genetic drift that mainly caused the fixation of one allele in all six copies, rather than absence of one genotype in one of the subgenomes leading to four copies of the <i>I. trifida</i>-like allele and two copies of the <i>I. triloba</i>-like allele. Interestingly, we seldom found “Tanzania” molecules specific to one progenitor type (&lt;1%; i.e., those with all reads supporting only the <i>I. trifida</i>-specific or <i>I. triloba</i>-specific alleles). Instead, the reads supporting the alleles from different homeologous subgenomes were interwoven within 10× molecules, suggesting recombination between the subgenomes. The proportion of <i>I. trifida</i> specific alleles within molecules peaked around 0.6 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">13c</a>), suggesting the approximate amount of contribution of the <i>I. trifida</i>-like progenitor genome to the hexaploid sweetpotato genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Facilitating genome-enabled breeding for subsistence farmers</h3><p>The Mwanga diversity panel (MDP) contains 16 sweetpotato cultivars, breeding lines and landraces selected from germplasm sourced from different areas across Uganda and a few selected introductions. The MDP accessions have been extensively used as parents in African sweetpotato breeding programs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="David, M. C. et al. Gene pool subdivision of East African sweetpotato parental material. Crop Sci. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.2135/cropsci2017.11.0695&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2087">23</a></sup>. Previous work using 31 simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers separated the 16 accessions into two genetic groups, population Uganda A and population Uganda B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="David, M. C. et al. Gene pool subdivision of East African sweetpotato parental material. Crop Sci. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.2135/cropsci2017.11.0695&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2091">23</a></sup>. To revisit their phylogeny and genetic diversity, and to reveal genes and alleles associated with agronomic traits, we resequenced these 16 accessions and aligned the reads to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> reference assemblies (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM9">6</a>). Due to the highly heterozygous nature of the subgenomes within hexaploid sweetpotato, especially within intergenic regions, accurate variant calling was restricted to genic regions, and a total of 6,138,575 and 6,722,808 high-confidence variant loci were identified in the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> reference assemblies, respectively, from these 16 accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">5</a>). For both reference genomes, approximately 95% of these loci were bi-allelic. Genotypes of the MDP accessions were consistent with a highly heterozygous genome in hexaploid <i>I. batatas</i>, with heterozygous calls at &gt;50% of SNP loci in each accession. The proportions of allele dosage were roughly equivalent among the MDP accessions using either reference genome, at roughly a 3:2:1 ratio for simplex to duplex to triplex dosage classes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">14a</a>, b).</p><p>SNP-based genetic clustering of the MDP accessions was similar, but inconsistent with their population membership determined previously using SSR markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="David, M. C. et al. Gene pool subdivision of East African sweetpotato parental material. Crop Sci. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.2135/cropsci2017.11.0695&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2123">23</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig4">4a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">14c</a>), and our results, derived using over one million bi-allelic SNPs with no missing calls for all MDP accessions, represent an improved delineation of their genetic relationships due to the higher resolution available with this high-density, robust polymorphic marker set. Accessions from population A formed two separate and highly supported clusters, whereas population B accessions did not cluster well, with “Magabali”, “NASPOT 11”, and “New Kawogo” forming a well-supported clade. Principal component analysis (PCA) of the 16 MDP accessions further supported their relationships (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">15a</a>), and population structure analysis revealed admixture in most of the 16 accessions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">15b</a>), which was not surprising as several of the lines used in the MDP crossing block can trace their parentage to landraces obtained from farmers in Uganda.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="930" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Genetic diversity of key African sweetpotato accessions. <b>a</b> Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of the Mwanga Diversity Panel accessions based on single nucleotide polymorphisms detected using the <i>I. trifida</i> genome as the reference. Population membership based on previous simple sequence repeat analysis is indicated by branch colors. Numbers at nodes indicate the percentage of 1000 bootstrap replications that support each clade. Huarmeyano was used as an outgroup for rooting. <b>b</b> Read depth analyses of “Beauregard” and “NASPOT 5” based on alignment to the <i>I. trifida</i> genome. For each box plot, the lower and upper bounds of the box indicate the first and third quartiles, respectively, and the center line indicates the median. <b>c</b> A metaphase cell of “Beauregard” showing 90 chromosomes and “NASPOT 5” showing 88 chromosomes. Bars represent 10 μm</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Median read depth was relatively stable from chromosome to chromosome for most accessions, but increased read depth on chromosome 1 initially suggested 6× +1 aneuploidy in “Huarmeyano” and “Mugande”, while decreased read depth on chromosomes 8 and 11 suggested 6× −2 aneuploidy in “NASPOT 5” (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">16</a>). There was no substantial decrease or increase in SNP density for the chromosomes with putative 6× +1 or 6× −2 changes, respectively, suggesting that these chromosomal aberrations in read depth are not likely due to differences in similarity to the reference genomes. Thus, we implemented a statistical test to detect aneuploidy based on read depth (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">7</a>). The test indicated that “NASPOT 5” had a ~0.9 loss of chromosome 8 (<i>p</i> &lt; 1.5e–36 for ≥5.13 copies) as well as a ~0.6 loss of chromosome 11 (<i>p</i> &lt; 4.7e–78 for ≥5.39 copies), and “Huarmeyano” had a ~0.3 gain on chromosome 1 (<i>p</i> &lt; 1e–20 for ≤6.3 copies) and “Mugande” a ~0.5 gain on chromosome 1 (<i>p</i> &lt; 3e–97 for ≤6.52 copies) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">17</a>). Consistent with our read mapping results, counts of chromosomes in root tip cells confirmed that “NASPOT 5” is a double monosomic line with 88 chromosomes, whereas “Mugande” contains 90 chromosomes (as do “Beauregard” and “Tanzania”), and thus, is not an aneuploid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig4">4c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">18</a>). Structural variation in the form of copy number and presence/absence variation have been reported previously in a wide range of crop species and have been associated with phenotypic variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cook, D. E. et al. Copy number variation of multiple genes at Rhg1 mediates nematode resistance in soybean. Science 338, 1206–1209 (2012)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2207">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Maron, L. G. et al. Aluminum tolerance in maize is associated with higher MATE1 gene copy number. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 5241–5246 (2013)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2207_1">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Genome-wide mapping of structural variations reveals a copy number variant that determines reproductive morphology in cucumber. Plant Cell 27, 1595–1604 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2210">26</a></sup>. Analyses in potato, another vegetatively propagated polyploid crop, revealed extensive structural variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hardigan, M. A. et al. Genome reduction uncovers a large dispensable genome and adaptive role for copy number variation in asexually propagated Solanum tuberosum. Plant Cell 28, 388–405 (2016)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2215">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hardigan, M. A. et al. Genome diversity of tuber-bearing Solanum uncovers complex evolutionary history and targets of domestication in the cultivated potato. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E9999–E10008 (2017)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2215_1">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Iovene, M., Zhang, T., Lou, Q., Buell, C. R. &amp; Jiang, J. Copy number variation in potato—an asexually propagated autotetraploid species. Plant J. 75, 80–89 (2013)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2215_2">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Pham, G. M. et al. Extensive genome heterogeneity leads to preferential allele expression and copy number-dependent expression in cultivated potato. Plant J. 92, 624–637 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2218">30</a></sup>, including presence/absence variation of sequences up to 575 kb in length that impacts transcript dosage. The discovery of aneuploidy in cultivated sweetpotato may present an extreme form of structural variation that clearly would affect transcript dosage and consequently, phenotypic variation.</p><p>Orange flesh in sweetpotato is correlated with high β-carotene content, and cultivation and consumption of OFSP has been promoted in SSA to help alleviate vitamin A deficiency<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Mwanga, R. O. M. &amp; Ssemakula, G. Orange-fleshed sweetpotatoes for food, health and wealth in Uganda. Int. J. Agric. Sustain. 9, 42–49 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2225">31</a></sup>. However, other traits such as yield, dry matter content, disease and insect resistance, and stress tolerance are important in development of consumer-accepted varieties. The MDP is composed of orange- and white-fleshed accessions (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM9">6</a>), and to enable stacking high β-carotene with other agronomic traits, SNPs associated with flesh color in the MDP were identified. The identified SNPs occurred in homologs of genes encoding key carotenoid biosynthetic enzymes, including phytoene synthase (PSY), phytoene desaturase (PDS), ζ-carotene isomerase (Z-ISO), and lycopene β-cyclase (LCYB) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig5">5a, b</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM10">7</a> and Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">8</a>). The <i>PSY</i> (<i>itf03g05110</i>), <i>PDS</i> (<i>itf11g08190</i>) and <i>Z-ISO</i> (<i>itf04g12320</i>) genes were of particular interest as they were upregulated in orange-fleshed ‘Beauregard’ storage roots as compared to other root types (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig5">5c</a>). In contrast, the <i>LCYB</i> (<i>itf04g32080</i>) gene had reduced expression in storage roots as compared to fibrous roots, suggesting it may not be involved in conferring orange flesh in “Beauregard” storage roots. Furthermore, three SNPs in <i>PSY</i> that were significantly associated with orange flesh in the MDP showed biased expression of the “orange” alleles in “Beauregard” storage roots at 40 and/or 50 days after transplanting (DAT) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">19</a>), suggesting that the basis of increased carotenoid accumulation in “Beauregard” is expression-based. One SNP associated with orange flesh in <i>PDS</i> was weakly significant for biased expression of the “orange” allele at 20 DAT yet not significant in 50 DAT storage roots, suggesting that expression of specific <i>PDS</i> alleles prior to storage root initiation might be important in the development of orange flesh. Together, our analysis identified logical control points for the accumulation of β-carotene in OFSP, and these loci provide targets for marker-assisted selection of orange-fleshed varieties.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="619" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Analysis of carotenoid metabolism in hexaploid sweetpotato. <b>a</b> Carotenoid biosynthesis and degradation pathways. Dashed arrows indicate multi-step reactions. Red arrows indicate degradation reactions. Red rectangles indicate components with significantly enriched SNPs at <i>p</i> &lt; 0.005 (Fisher’s exact test). PSY, phytoene synthase; PDS phytoene desaturase; Z-ISO ζ-carotene isomerase; ZDS ζ-carotene desaturase; CRTISO carotenoid isomerase; LCYE lycopene ε-cyclase; LCYB lycopene β-cyclase; β-OHase β-ring hydroxylase; CYP cytochrome P450; VDE violaxanthin de-epoxidase; ZEP zeaxanthin epoxidase; CCD carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase. <b>b</b> Fisher’s exact test for differences in allele frequencies between orange and white-fleshed MDP accessions for putative carotenoid biosynthesis loci. Each color within a chromosome indicates SNPs from the same gene. The dotted and solid lines indicate <i>p</i> = 0.005 and <i>p</i> = 0.05, respectively. <b>c</b> Expression profiles of genes involved in carotenoid biosynthesis in different types of roots of ‘Beauregard’ during development. Gene-wise <i>Z</i>-scores were calculated from the arithmetic means of replicates after log2 transformation of FPKM values plus 1. DAT days after transplanting; Undiff undifferentiated</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Origins and evolution of the Batatas complex</h3><p>Phylogenetic inferences attempting to describe the evolutionary history of the Batatas complex have been highly incongruent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Austin, D. F. in Exploration, Maintenance and Utilization of Sweet Potato Genetic Resources. Report of the First Sweet Potato Planning Conference (International Potato Centre (CIP), Lima, Peru, 1987) (ed. P., G.) 27–59 (CIP, 1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2331">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2334">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Jarret, R. L., Gawel, N. &amp; Whittemore, A. Phylogenetic relationships of the sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.]. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 117, 633–637 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2337">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Rajapakse, S. et al. Phylogenetic relationships of the sweetpotato in Ipomoea series Batatas (Convolvulaceae) based on nuclear β-amylase gene sequences. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 30, 623–632 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2340">33</a></sup>, yet no study to date has explicitly tested for hybridization or reticulate evolution within the complex. Rather than a strictly bifurcating tree, the true evolutionary relationships among species or populations within the Batatas complex may be best understood as a network with reticulations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Huson, D. H. &amp; Bryant, D. Application of phylogenetic networks in evolutionary studies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 254–267 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2344">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Yu, Y., Dong, J., Liu, K. J. &amp; Nakhleh, L. Maximum likelihood inference of reticulate evolutionary histories. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 16448–16453 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2347">35</a></sup>. To test the hypothesis of recent or ancient hybridization in the Batatas complex, we performed targeted sequence capture on 490 genes that are single copy in the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genomes as well as genome assemblies of other core eudicots as described in <i>Amborella</i> Genome Project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2361">36</a></sup>. We first included twenty-six accessions in a species-tree analysis (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM11">8</a>), representing the breadth of phenotypic diversity across the Batatas complex. Our sample included <i>I. triloba</i> and <i>I. trifida</i> accessions with genome sequences (NCNSP0323 and NCNSP0306; ITR and ITRk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 22, 171–179 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2377">14</a></sup>), and twelve polyploid <i>I. batatas</i> accessions. Haplotype diversity was assessed for each gene within each of the <i>I. batatas</i> assemblies and only genes with a single haplotype (i.e., nulliplex) or two haplotypes that were sister in the gene trees were included in phylogenetic analyses. The coalescence-based species-tree estimation did not account for reticulations in the phylogeny, but nonetheless recovered six distinct lineages within the Batatas complex (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig6">6a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">20</a>). <i>Ipomoea triloba</i> accessions formed a clade, whereas relationships could not be resolved within a clade including all <i>I. trifida</i> and cultivated sweetpotato (<i>I</i>. <i>batatas</i>) accessions, and an unnamed diploid accession. Another unnamed diploid accession was recovered as sister to all other members of the Batatas complex aside from <i>I. splendor-sylvae</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig6">6a</a>). While there was no overlap in accessions used in this study and a recently published analysis of Batatas complex species relationships based on 307 exon-capture assemblies and the whole plastid genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2413">6</a></sup>, both analyses infer early divergence of ancestral lineages for <i>I</i>. <i>splendor-sylvae</i> and the remainder of the Batatas complex, followed by divergence of lineages leading the last common ancestors of accessions in the <i>I</i>. <i>batatas</i> + <i>I</i>. <i>trifida</i> clade and the <i>I</i>. <i>triloba</i> + <i>I</i>. <i>lacunosa</i> + <i>I</i>. <i>cordatotriloba</i> + <i>I</i>. <i>tenuissima</i> clade.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-06983-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_6983_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="479" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Phylogenetic relationships among members of the Batatas complex. <b>a</b> Phylogenetic relationships among Batatas complex accessions inferred using the species-tree method ASTRAL-II. Bootstrap support values are shown next to the branches. Relationships among <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. batatas</i> accessions were not resolved. <b>b</b> Phylogenetic network of the Batatas complex including pseudolikelihood estimates for hybridization events in the ancestor of red, green and yellow clades as well as in the ancestry of cultivated sweetpotato.
