Blue lines indicate the two-reticulation events inferred in PhyloNet. Below the phylogenetic network are −log-likelihood scores for the tree generated in PhyloNet under the zero-reticulation model (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">20d</a>) and the network estimated under the two-reticulation model. Color bars at the right of each tree denote the six inferred major lineages</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Reticulations in the phylogeny were tested using pseudolikelihood method for inferring phylogenetic networks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Than, C., Ruths, D. &amp; Nakhleh, L. PhyloNet: a software package for analyzing and reconstructing reticulate evolutionary relationships. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 322 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2498">37</a></sup>. The PhyloNet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Than, C., Ruths, D. &amp; Nakhleh, L. PhyloNet: a software package for analyzing and reconstructing reticulate evolutionary relationships. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 322 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2502">37</a></sup> analysis was run using a single exemplar for polyploid <i>I. batatas</i> together with all of the diploid Batatas complex accessions used in the species-tree analysis. The likelihood of a network with two reticulations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig6">6b</a>) was significantly better than a tree with the same samples estimated without reticulations (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.001, likelihood ratio test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">20d</a>), but otherwise the relationships among cultivated sweetpotato (using ‘Tinian’ as an exemplar), <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> remained unchanged. The reticulation giving rise to cultivated sweetpotato exhibited evidence of a wide ancestral cross. However, since the taxonomy of accession PI 552786 collected from Bolivia is uncertain, the results do not clearly favor conspecific parents (autopolyploidy) or interspecific hybridization (allopolyploidy) within the <i>I. trifida</i> + <i>I. batatas</i> clade as giving rise to polyploid <i>I. batatas</i>. The deeper reticulation in the PhyloNet network suggests that extensive gene flow between divergent ancestral populations gave rise to the clade including <i>I. triloba, I. lacunosa, I. cordatotriloba</i> and <i>I</i>. <i>tenuissima</i>. With respect to <i>I. batatas</i> “Tinian”, the inference that parental lineages were genetically divergent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig6">6b</a>) was consistent with results of mapping the <i>I. batatas</i> “Tanzania” genomic reads to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> reference genomes and allele frequency analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">20</a>). This finding underscores the continuum between autopolyploidy and allopolyploidy in outcrossing species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Sweetpotato is an important food security crop with a highly recognized potential to alleviate hunger, vitamin A deficiency, and poverty in SSA. We sequenced the genomes of two diploid relatives of sweetpotato, <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i>, and demonstrated that these high-quality assemblies can be used as robust references to facilitate sweetpotato breeding. While <i>I. trifida</i> is the more closely related diploid to hexaploid sweetpotato, and is a better reference for sweetpotato, the <i>I. triloba</i> genome can be a complementary reference sequence.</p><p>Many species in the Batatas complex are able to hybridize with one another, and hybridization seems to be important in the evolution of Batatas complex populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Abel, W. E. &amp; Austin, D. F. Introgressive hybridization between Ipomoea trichocarpa and Ipomoea lacunosa (Convolvulaceae). Bull. Torrey Bot. Club 108, 231–239 (1981)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2590">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Diaz, J., Schmiediche, P. &amp; Austin, D. F. Polygon of crossability between eleven species of Ipomoea: section Batatas (Convolvulaceae). Euphytica 88, 189–200 (1996)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2590_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Cao, Q. et al. Novel interspecific hybridization between sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) and its two diploid wild relatives. Euphytica 169, 345–352 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2593">40</a></sup>. Some diploid species within the complex have been hypothesized as hybrid species, and sweetpotato is thought to have arisen through either allopolyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Austin, D. F. in Exploration, Maintenance and Utilization of Sweet Potato Genetic Resources. Report of the First Sweet Potato Planning Conference (International Potato Centre (CIP), Lima, Peru, 1987) (ed. P., G.) 27–59 (CIP, 1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2597">4</a></sup>, autopolyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Muñoz-Rodríguez, P. et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr. Biol. 28, 1246–1256 (2018). e12." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2601">6</a></sup>, or both<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kriegner, A., Cervantes, J. C., Burg, K., Mwanga, R. O. M. &amp; Zhang, D. A genetic linkage map of sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.] based on AFLP markers. Mol. Breed. 11, 169–185 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2605">10</a></sup>. Furthermore, microsatellite data provide evidence supporting on-going gene flow between sympatric populations of <i>I. cordatotriloba</i> and <i>I. lacunosa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Duncan, T. M. &amp; Rausher, M. D. Morphological and genetic differentiation and reproductive isolation among closely related taxa in the Ipomoea series Batatas. Am. J. Bot. 100, 2183–2193 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2615">41</a></sup>. The observation that many species in the Batatas complex appear morphologically similar and show evidence of hybridization may be the result of ongoing or ancient hybridization. Results from read mapping and phylogenetic analyses suggest that cultivated sweetpotato is derived from a cross between divergent parents but their taxonomy is equivocal.</p><p>Comparative analyses using the diploid genome sequences revealed an ancient WGT event specific to the <i>Ipomoea</i> genus. We identified gene families specifically expanded in <i>I. trifida</i> and/or <i>I. triloba</i> associated with adaptation and stress resistance in roots including sporamin genes, which could be a prerequisite for storage root development in the hexaploid sweetpotato. Genome resequencing of 16 sweetpotato cultivars and landraces used in African breeding programs allowed refinement of the genetic relationships among these key accessions, an understanding of dosage distribution within cultivated sweetpotato, and identification of genes and alleles associated with high β-carotene content, highlighting how genomic tools can enable more efficient improvement of sweetpotato. We anticipate that these resources will become increasingly valuable for the global sweetpotato breeding and genomic community as we seek to further improve this critical food crop using genome-enabled marker-assisted breeding methods.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">De novo genome assembly</h3><p>For <i>I. triloba</i> NCNSP0323, high-quality cleaned Illumina paired-end and mate-pair reads (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>) were assembled into scaffolds with SOAPdenovo2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2649">42</a></sup>) and gaps in the resulting scaffolds were filled with GapCloser in the SOAPdenovo2 package. For <i>I. trifida</i> NCNSP0306, cleaned paired-end and mate-pair reads were assembled into scaffolds using Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384–1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2656">43</a></sup> (v1.2.1). The error-corrected PacBio long reads were used to further fill gaps in the scaffolds and to connect scaffolds using PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with pacific biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2661">44</a></sup> (v15.8.24). Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2665">45</a></sup> (v1.13) was used to improve the assembly by correcting base errors, fixing misassemblies, and further filling gaps. Potential contamination from microorganisms was detected by aligning the assemblies to the NCBI nonredundant nucleotide (nt) database using BLASTN with an <i>e</i> value cutoff of 1e−5. Scaffolds with more than 90% of their length similar to only bacterial or virus sequences were considered contaminants and removed. Finally, scaffolds contained within other scaffolds with sequence identity &gt;99% and coverage &gt;99% were removed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>Custom repeat libraries were individually created for <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> by combining the putative repeat libraries predicted from MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2686">46</a></sup> (v2011) and RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>; v1.0.8). Protein-coding genes were removed from the repeat library using ProtExcluder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2697">47</a></sup>. The Repbase (v20150807) repeats for green plants (Viridiplantae) were then added to each library to create a final custom repeat library for each species. The pseudomolecules for <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> were repeat masked with the respective repeat library using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>; v4.0.6).</p><p>For gene prediction, AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465–W467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2718">48</a></sup> (v3.1) was trained on the soft-masked assemblies using the leaf RNA-Seq alignments. Gene models were predicted with AUGUSTUS using the hard-masked assemblies and refined with PASA2 (v2.0.2) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2722">49</a></sup>) using the genome-guided transcript assemblies from each tissue as transcript evidence (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">1</a>). Two rounds of gene prediction comparison were performed and gene models PASA identified as merged, but unable to split, were manually inspected and split as necessary. A third round of gene prediction comparison was performed to refine the structure of the manually curated models. The final output from PASA2 is the working set of gene models. Expression abundances for each gene model were determined based on the RNA-Seq read alignments using Cufflinks2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential analysis of gene regulation at transcript resolution with RNA-seq. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 46–53 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2729">50</a></sup>) (v.2.2.1). A high-confidence gene model set was constructed from the working gene model set by removing partial gene models and gene models with an internal stop codon, a hit to a transposable element, or an FPKM of 0 across the RNA-Seq libraries used for the annotation.</p><p>Functional annotations for the high-confidence gene models were assigned by comparing their protein sequences against the Arabidopsis proteome (TAIR 10), Pfam (v29), and the Swiss-Prot databases. Proteins that only matched a hypothetical Arabidopsis gene model and had no matches in the other databases were annotated as conserved hypothetical, while proteins with no matches in any of the databases were annotated as hypothetical.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Comparative genomics analysis and gene family expansions</h3><p>OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2744">51</a></sup> was used to infer homology among proteins from <i>I. batatas</i> (derived from the “Beauregard” transcriptome assembly; Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">4</a>), <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba</i>, and seven additional plant species: <i>I. nil</i> (v1.1), potato (<i>S. tuberosum</i>; v3.4), tomato (<i>S. lycopersicum</i>; ITAG2.4), grape (<i>V. vinifera</i>; Genoscope 12×), Arabidopsis (<i>A. thaliana</i>; TAIR10), rice (<i>O. sativa</i>; v7.0), and Amborella (<i>A. trichopoda</i>; v1.0).</p><p>Lineage-specific gene family expansion was inferred from orthologous groups using the software CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2786">52</a></sup> (v3.0). <i>I. batatas</i> was excluded from the analysis because its transcriptome assembly did not represent the complete gene content. The random gene birth and death rates were estimated using a maximum likelihood method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2793">52</a></sup> across the species tree composed of the nine species. Orthologous groups with accelerated rate of expansion in <i>I. trifida</i> and/or <i>I. triloba</i> were determined with a branch-specific Viterbi <i>p</i> value cutoff of 0.01. Given that CAFE may fail to provide accurate lambda estimates for gene families with large differences in size among species, we also considered gene families expanded if they contained more than ten genes in either <i>I. trifida</i> or <i>I. triloba</i> and at least 50% more genes in these two species than any of the other investigated species. Fisher’s exact test followed by a Bonferroni multiple test correction was used to determine gene enrichment within 96 gene ontology terms as well as for tissue specific, biotic and abiotic stress conditions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Synteny and whole-genome duplication analysis</h3><p>To identify syntenic regions within and between genomes, protein sequences of <i>I. trifida</i>, <i>I. triloba</i>, <i>I. nil</i>, tomato, and potato were self-aligned and aligned with each other using BLASTP. High-confidence collinear blocks were determined using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2830">53</a></sup> with an <i>e</i> value cutoff of 1e−10. <i>K</i>s values of homologous pairs were calculated using the Yang–Nielsen algorithm implemented in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Bioinformatics 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2841">54</a></sup>. Divergence time (<i>T</i>) was calculated as <i>T</i> = <i>K</i>s/2r, where <i>r</i> is the evolutionary rate (7.0 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions/site/year), according to those reported in the Solanaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Moniz de Sa, M. &amp; Drouin, G. Phylogeny and substitution rates of angiosperm actin genes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 13, 1198–1212 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2860">20</a></sup> (6.96 × 10<sup>−9</sup>) and Arabidopsis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2866">21</a></sup> (7.0 × 10<sup>−9</sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">10× Genomics data analysis</h3><p>We generated 10× Genomics sequences for hexaploid sweetpotato cultivar “Tanzania” (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">6</a>). To reconstruct “Tanzania” molecules sequenced by the 10× platform, we grouped the reads by barcodes. For each group, we then identified clusters of linked reads, which were mapped to the same region in the reference genome. Two consecutively mapped reads were considered linked (i.e., sequenced from the same “Tanzania” molecule) if their barcodes were identical and the alignment distance between them was ≤10 kb. Each linked read cluster was regarded as a span of a “Tanzania” molecule. Only clusters spanning at least 10 kb in the reference genome were retained. It should be noted that, given the aforementioned method for the molecule reconstruction, each “Tanzania” molecule represented a homologous region between the “Tanzania” genome and the diploid genome that was used as the reference. If a “Tanzania” molecule is homologous to both diploid genomes, i.e., the two linked read clusters for a “Tanzania” molecule obtained from using the two diploid reference genomes are identical, then we considered it to be a three-way homology meaning the homologous region was shared between the “Tanzania” genome and the two diploid genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Variant detection and phylogeny of hexaploid sweetpotatoes</h3><p>Whole genome sequence reads from the 16 MDP accessions (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">7</a>) were cleaned using Cutadapt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Martin, M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. EMBnet. J. 17, 10–12 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2895">55</a></sup> (v1.11) with a base quality threshold of 20 and a minimum length of 200 bases after trimming. Reads were aligned to the <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> genome assemblies using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997v2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2905">56</a></sup> (v0.7.12) in paired-end mode with default parameters. Picard tools (v2.7.1; <a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard</a>) were used to remove duplicate read pairs and alignments with a MAPQ score less than 30. Alignments were refined using the GATK IndelRealigner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2917">57</a></sup> (v3.6.0). Alignments to the same reference assembly from all individuals were analyzed together for variant and genotype calling at regions overlapping genes only. FreeBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2921">58</a></sup> (v1.1.0) was used with an assumed ploidy of 6, “haplotype-length” of 0, minimum base quality of 20, and a minimum of 2 reads and 10% of the reads in at least one single individual supporting an alternate allele. Variant records were filtered using the “vcffilter” function in vcflib (v1.0.0; <a href="https://github.com/vcflib/vcflib">https://github.com/vcflib/vcflib</a>) with a minimum variant quality score of 40, “SAF” &gt; 1, “SAR” &gt; 1, “RPR” &gt; 1, and “RPL” &gt; 1. Individual genotypes were filtered using a minimum depth of 18, maximum depths based on average depth across genic regions plus 2 standard deviations for each sample, and a minimum of 5% of reads supporting the least frequent allele in a genotype call. Complex variants were decomposed to SNPs and indels using the “vcfallelicprimitives” function in vcflib and variant records were removed if all the genotypes containing the alternate allele failed the above quality filters. All analyses were performed using these filtered variant and genotype calls, with additional processing as described.</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis was performed using bi-allelic SNPs with no missing data for any accessions, with Rphylip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Revell, L. J. &amp; Chamberlain, S. A. Rphylip: an R interface for PHYLIP. Methods Ecol. Evol. 5, 976–981 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2935">59</a></sup> (v0.1–23). Nei’s genetic distances were calculated using ‘gendist’ and a neighbor-joining tree was constructed using ‘neighbor’. Bootstrap analysis was done using 1000 replications created with ‘boot.phylo’ from the R package, ape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Paradis, E., Claude, J. &amp; Strimmer, K. APE: analyses of phylogenetics and evolution in R language. Bioinformatics 20, 289–290 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2939">60</a></sup> (v4.1). PCA analysis was performed using PLINK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: A tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559–575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2943">61</a></sup> (v1.9) and population structure analysis using STRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Falush, D., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data: linked loci and correlated allele frequencies. Genetics 164, 1567–1587 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2947">62</a></sup> (v2.3.4). The most likely number of clusters (<i>K</i>) was estimated by calculating delta <i>K</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software structure: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611–2620 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2957">63</a></sup>. STRUCTURE analyses were run 20 times for each <i>K</i> value ranging from 1 to 10, using 8,000 randomly selected SNPs with an admixture model. The subgroup memberships of accessions were determined based on 10,000 iterations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenomic analysis of species in the Batatas complex</h3><p>We performed targeted sequence capture on 490 single-copy genes for fifteen accessions in the Batatas complex and computationally extracted target exon sequences from available genome assemblies for <i>I. trifida</i> (NCNSP0306, ITR and ITRk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 22, 171–179 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2975">14</a></sup>) and <i>I. triloba</i> (NCNSP0323), and from shotgun reads from 18 <i>I. batatas</i> accessions (Taizhong6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, J. et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat. Plants 3, 696–703 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2985">15</a></sup><sub>,</sub> Tanzania, and 16 MDP accessions) (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">9</a>). Multiple sequence alignment for each single-copy gene family was performed using PRANK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Löytynoja, A. &amp; Goldman, N. Phylogeny-aware gap placement prevents errors in sequence alignment and evolutionary analysis. Science 320, 1632–1635 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2994">64</a></sup>, and alignments were filtered using Gblocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Talavera, G. &amp; Castresana, J. Improvement of phylogenies after removing divergent and ambiguously aligned blocks from protein sequence alignments. Syst. Biol. 56, 564–577 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d6145e2998">65</a></sup> to remove poorly aligned regions. Gene trees were estimated separately for each gene alignment in RAxML with 100 bootstraps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d6145e3002">66</a></sup> (v8.0.0). For each gene, samples exhibiting more than a single-hapotype or an accession-specific clade in the gene tree were removed (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#MOESM1">9</a>). After filtering, 11 hexaploid <i>I. batatas</i> accessions had fewer than 30 retained single-hapotype genes and were removed from further analysis. A majority of the remaining 12 <i>I. batatas</i> accessions were retained in 85 gene trees. These single-copy/single-haploid gene trees were included in ASTRAL-II<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Mirarab, S. &amp; Warnow, T. ASTRAL-II: coalescent-based species tree estimation with many hundreds of taxa and thousands of genes. Bioinformatics 31, i44–i52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d6145e3016">67</a></sup> (v4.10.12) and SVDQuartets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Chifman, J. &amp; Kubatko, L. Quartet inference from SNP data under the coalescent model. Bioinformatics 30, 3317–3324 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d6145e3020">68</a></sup> estimates of relationships among the Batatas-complex species and accessions while accounting for incomplete lineage sorting.</p><p>PhyloNet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Than, C., Ruths, D. &amp; Nakhleh, L. PhyloNet: a software package for analyzing and reconstructing reticulate evolutionary relationships. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 322 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-06983-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6145e3027">37</a></sup> was used to infer a phylogenetic network while accounting for incomplete lineage sorting under zero, one, two, and three reticulation scenarios using the InferNetwork_MPL option and gene tree from RAxML as the input. Five independent analyses of each reticulation scenario were carried out to best traverse the complex parameter space. The phylogenetic network with the best likelihood score, which was significantly different from the likelihood of a zero-reticulation network (i.e., the ASTRAL-II and SVDQuartets trees) was chosen as the most likely network given the gene trees. PhyloNet is computationally intensive, so only the ‘Tinian’ accession was included as an exemplar for <i>I. batatas</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome assemblies and raw genome reads of <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. triloba</i> have been deposited into GenBank BioProject under accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA428214/">PRJNA428214</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA428241/">PRJNA428241</a>, respectively. Raw 10x Genomics, genome resequencing and RNA-Seq reads are available in the National Center for Biotechnology Information Sequence Read Archive under accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP161954">SRP161954</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP162006">SRP162006</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP132113">SRP132113</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP132112">SRP132112</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP162112">SRP162112</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP162110">SRP162110</a>, and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP162021">SRP162021</a>. The pseudomolecules, genome annotation, expression abundances, BLAST server, and Jbrowse instance are available in the Sweetpotato Genomics Resource (<a href="http://sweetpotato.plantbiology.msu.edu">http://sweetpotato.plantbiology.msu.edu</a>). In addition, the genome assemblies, annotated genes, expression abundances, and variants of the MDP are available via the Dryad Digital Repository (<a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.b9m61cg">https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.b9m61cg</a>). Results of phylogenetic analysis and the associated multiple sequence alignments were available in the Github repository (<a href="https://github.com/laeserman/Wu-et-al.-2018-Nature-Comm">https://github.com/laeserman/Wu-et-al.-2018-Nature-Comm</a>).</p>

G.C.Y, R.O.M.M., C.J., D.C.G., A.K., M.K., M.G., G.T.G. and K.W.-R. prepared samples and extracted DNA/RNA for genome and transcriptome sequencing. Q.C., D.M., X.Y. J.S., G.C.Y. and Z.F. contributed to genome and transcriptome sequencing. S.W., H.S., Q.C. and K.B. performed genome assembly and comparative genomic analysis. J.P.H., G.T.G., E.C., B.V., K.W-R. and S.W. performed genome annotation and transcriptome analysis. K.H.L., J.P.H., N.M-C., X.W. and L.J.M.C conducted analysis of genome resequencing data and population genomics analysis. W.G., B.A.O., D.C.G., A.K. and C.Z. developed and sequenced the diploid mapping population and constructed the genetic map. C.Z. and L.J.M.C. generated and analyzed the 10× Genomics data. L.E. and J.L.M. conducted targeted sequencing and phylogenomic analysis. H.W. and J.J. performed chromosome counting. S.W., K.H.L., Z.F. and C.R.B. wrote the paper. All authors read and approved the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
